<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Conic section</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Curve obtained by intersecting a cone and a plane</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TypesOfConicSections.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/TypesOfConicSections.jpg/220px-TypesOfConicSections.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/TypesOfConicSections.jpg/330px-TypesOfConicSections.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/TypesOfConicSections.jpg/440px-TypesOfConicSections.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TypesOfConicSections.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Types of conic sections:<br/> <span class="nowrap">1: <a href="/wiki/Circle" title="Circle">Circle</a></span>       <span class="nowrap">2: <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">Ellipse</a></span><br/><span class="nowrap">3: <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">Parabola</a></span>  <span class="nowrap">4: <a href="/wiki/Hyperbola" title="Hyperbola">Hyperbola</a></span></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Table_of_Conics,_Cyclopaedia,_volume_1,_p_304,_1728.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2614" data-file-width="1642" decoding="async" height="350" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Table_of_Conics%2C_Cyclopaedia%2C_volume_1%2C_p_304%2C_1728.jpg/220px-Table_of_Conics%2C_Cyclopaedia%2C_volume_1%2C_p_304%2C_1728.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Table_of_Conics%2C_Cyclopaedia%2C_volume_1%2C_p_304%2C_1728.jpg/330px-Table_of_Conics%2C_Cyclopaedia%2C_volume_1%2C_p_304%2C_1728.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Table_of_Conics%2C_Cyclopaedia%2C_volume_1%2C_p_304%2C_1728.jpg/440px-Table_of_Conics%2C_Cyclopaedia%2C_volume_1%2C_p_304%2C_1728.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Table_of_Conics,_Cyclopaedia,_volume_1,_p_304,_1728.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Table of conics, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclopaedia,_or_an_Universal_Dictionary_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Cyclopaedia, or an Universal Dictionary of Arts and Sciences">Cyclopaedia</a></i>, 1728</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, a <b>conic section</b>, <b>quadratic curve</b> or simply <b>conic</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Curve" title="Curve">curve</a> obtained as the intersection of the <a href="/wiki/Conical_surface" title="Conical surface">surface</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cone_(geometry)" title="Cone (geometry)">cone</a> with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plane_(mathematics)" title="Plane (mathematics)">plane</a>. The three types of conic section are the <a href="/wiki/Hyperbola" title="Hyperbola">hyperbola</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Circle" title="Circle">circle</a> is a special case of the ellipse, though historically it was sometimes called a fourth type. The <a href="/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">ancient Greek mathematicians</a> studied conic sections, culminating around 200 BC with <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Perga" title="Apollonius of Perga">Apollonius of Perga</a>'s systematic work on their properties.
</p><p>The conic sections in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euclidean_plane" title="Euclidean plane">Euclidean plane</a> have various distinguishing properties, many of which can be used as alternative definitions.  One such property defines a non-circular conic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> to be the <a href="/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set (mathematics)">set</a> of those points whose distances to some particular point, called a <i><a href="/wiki/Focus_(geometry)" title="Focus (geometry)">focus</a></i>, and some particular line, called a <i>directrix</i>, are in a fixed ratio, called the <i><a href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(mathematics)" title="Eccentricity (mathematics)">eccentricity</a></i>. The type of conic is determined by the value of the eccentricity. In <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">analytic geometry</a>, a conic may be defined as a <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_curve" title="Algebraic curve">plane algebraic curve</a> of degree 2; that is, as the set of points whose coordinates satisfy a <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_equation" title="Quadratic equation">quadratic equation</a> in two variables which can be written in the form <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle Ax^{2}+Bxy+Cy^{2}+Dx+Ey+F=0.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<msup>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle Ax^{2}+Bxy+Cy^{2}+Dx+Ey+F=0.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c87dffa2f0e5e3eef9856689d301dc7bb5e12b51" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:39.393ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> The geometric properties of the conic can be deduced from its equation.
</p><p>In the Euclidean plane, the three types of conic sections appear quite different, but share many properties. By extending the Euclidean plane to include a line at infinity, obtaining a <a href="/wiki/Projective_plane" title="Projective plane">projective plane</a>, the apparent difference vanishes: the branches of a hyperbola meet in two points at infinity, making it a single closed curve; and the two ends of a parabola meet to make it a closed curve tangent to the line at infinity. Further extension, by expanding the <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real</a> coordinates to admit <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex</a> coordinates, provides the means to see this unification algebraically.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Euclidean_geometry">Euclidean geometry</span></h2>
<p>The conic sections have been studied for thousands of years and have provided a rich source of interesting and beautiful results in <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Conic_Sections.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Conic_Sections.svg/220px-Conic_Sections.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Conic_Sections.svg/330px-Conic_Sections.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Conic_Sections.svg/440px-Conic_Sections.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Conic_Sections.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The black boundaries of the colored regions are conic sections. Not shown is the other half of the hyperbola, which is on the unshown other half of the double cone.</div></div></div>
<p>A conic is the curve obtained as the intersection of a <a href="/wiki/Plane_(geometry)" title="Plane (geometry)">plane</a>, called the <i>cutting plane</i>, with the surface of a double <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cone_(geometry)" title="Cone (geometry)">cone</a> (a cone with two <i>nappes</i>). It is usually assumed that the cone is a right circular cone for the purpose of easy description, but this is not required; any double cone with some circular cross-section will suffice. Planes that pass through the vertex of the cone will intersect the cone in a point, a line or a pair of intersecting lines. These are called <a href="#Degenerate_cases"><i>degenerate</i> conics</a> and some authors do not consider them to be conics at all. Unless otherwise stated, "conic" in this article will refer to a non-degenerate conic.
</p><p>There are three types of conics: the <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hyperbola" title="Hyperbola">hyperbola</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Circle" title="Circle">circle</a> is a special kind of ellipse, although historically Apollonius considered it a fourth type. Ellipses arise when the intersection of the cone and plane is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed_curve" title="Closed curve">closed curve</a>. The circle is obtained when the cutting plane is parallel to the plane of the generating circle of the cone; for a right cone, this means the cutting plane is perpendicular to the axis. If the cutting plane is <a href="/wiki/Parallel_(geometry)" title="Parallel (geometry)">parallel</a> to exactly one generating line of the cone, then the conic is unbounded and is called a <i>parabola</i>. In the remaining case, the figure is a <i>hyperbola</i>: the plane intersects <i>both</i> halves of the cone, producing two separate unbounded curves.
</p><p>Compare also <a href="/wiki/Circle_of_a_sphere" title="Circle of a sphere">spheric section</a> (intersection of a plane with a sphere, producing a circle or point), and <a href="/wiki/Spherical_conic" title="Spherical conic">spherical conic</a> (intersection of an elliptic cone with a concentric sphere).
</p>
<h3><span id="Eccentricity.2C_focus_and_directrix"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Eccentricity,_focus_and_directrix">Eccentricity, focus and directrix</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eccentricity.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="455" decoding="async" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Eccentricity.svg/280px-Eccentricity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Eccentricity.svg/420px-Eccentricity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Eccentricity.svg/560px-Eccentricity.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eccentricity.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ellipse (<i>e</i> = 1/2), parabola (<i>e</i> = 1) and hyperbola <span class="nowrap">(<i>e</i> = 2)</span> with fixed focus <i>F</i> and directrix  <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">L</span> (<i>e</i> = ∞). The red circle (<i>e</i> = 0) is included for reference; it does not have a directrix in the plane.</div></div></div>
<p>Alternatively, one can define a conic section purely in terms of plane geometry: it is the <a href="/wiki/Locus_(mathematics)" title="Locus (mathematics)">locus</a> of all points <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">P</span> whose distance to a fixed point <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">F</span> (called the <i><a href="/wiki/Focus_(geometry)" title="Focus (geometry)">focus</a></i>) is a constant multiple (called the <i><a href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(mathematics)" title="Eccentricity (mathematics)">eccentricity</a></i> <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">e</span>) of the distance from <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">P</span> to a fixed line <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">L</span> (called the <i>directrix</i>).
For <span class="texhtml">0 &lt; <i>e</i> &lt; 1</span> we obtain an ellipse, for <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i> = 1</span> a parabola, and for <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i> &gt; 1</span> a hyperbola.
</p><p>A circle is a limiting case and is not defined by a focus and directrix in the Euclidean plane. The eccentricity of a circle is defined to be zero and its focus is the center of the circle, but its directrix can only be taken as the line at infinity in the projective plane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The eccentricity of an ellipse can be seen as a measure of how far the ellipse deviates from being circular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>If the angle between the surface of the cone and its axis is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \beta }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>β<!-- β --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \beta }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\beta " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7ed48a5e36207156fb792fa79d29925d2f7901e8" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.332ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> and the angle between the cutting plane and the axis is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \alpha ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>α<!-- α --></mi>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>

<semantics>

<mi>β<!-- β --></mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {\cos \alpha }{\cos \beta }}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\frac {\cos \alpha }{\cos \beta }}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0eefbac290198f8c31062c5907db94bd54511268" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:6.469ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>A proof that the above curves defined by the <a href="/wiki/Dandelin_spheres#Proof_of_the_focus-directrix_property" title="Dandelin spheres">focus-directrix property</a> are the same as those obtained by planes intersecting a cone is facilitated by the use of <a href="/wiki/Dandelin_spheres" title="Dandelin spheres">Dandelin spheres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alternatively, an ellipse can be defined in terms of two focus points, as the locus of points for which the sum of the distances to the two foci is <span class="texhtml">2<i>a</i></span>; while a hyperbola is the locus for which the difference of distances is <span class="texhtml">2<i>a</i></span>. (Here <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i></span> is the semi-major axis defined below.) A parabola may also be defined in terms of its focus and latus rectum line (parallel to the directrix and passing through the focus): it is the locus of points whose distance to the focus plus or minus the distance to the line is equal to <span class="texhtml">2<i>a</i></span>; plus if the point is between the directrix and the latus rectum, minus otherwise.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conic_parameters">Conic parameters</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ellipse_parameters_2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="251" data-file-width="424" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Ellipse_parameters_2.svg/280px-Ellipse_parameters_2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Ellipse_parameters_2.svg/420px-Ellipse_parameters_2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Ellipse_parameters_2.svg/560px-Ellipse_parameters_2.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ellipse_parameters_2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Conic parameters in the case of an ellipse</div></div></div>
<p>In addition to the eccentricity (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">e</span>), foci, and directrix, various geometric features and lengths are associated with a conic section.
</p><p>The <i>principal axis</i> is the line joining the foci of an ellipse or hyperbola, and its midpoint is the curve's <i>center</i>. A parabola has no center.
</p><p>The <i>linear eccentricity</i> (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">c</span>) is the distance between the center and a focus.
</p><p>The <i>latus rectum</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Chord_(geometry)" title="Chord (geometry)">chord</a> parallel to the directrix and passing through a focus; its half-length is the <i>semi-latus rectum</i> (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">ℓ</span>).
</p><p>The <i>focal parameter</i> (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">p</span>) is the distance from a focus to the corresponding directrix.
</p><p>The <i>major axis</i> is the chord between the two vertices: the longest chord of an ellipse, the shortest chord between the branches of a hyperbola. Its half-length is the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a></i> (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span>). When an ellipse or hyperbola are in standard position as in the equations below, with foci on the <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">x</span>-axis and center at the origin, the vertices of the conic have coordinates <span class="texhtml">(−<i>a</i>, 0)</span> and <span class="texhtml">(<i>a</i>, 0)</span>, with <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span> non-negative.
</p><p>The <i>minor axis</i> is the shortest diameter of an ellipse, and its half-length is the <i>semi-minor axis</i> (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span>), the same value <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span> as in the standard equation below. By analogy, for a hyperbola the parameter <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span> in the standard equation is also called the semi-minor axis.
</p><p>The following relations hold:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>

</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_forms_in_Cartesian_coordinates">Standard forms in Cartesian coordinates</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_an_ellipse.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="312" data-file-width="468" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_an_ellipse.png/280px-Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_an_ellipse.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_an_ellipse.png/420px-Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_an_ellipse.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_an_ellipse.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_an_ellipse.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Standard forms of an ellipse</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_a_parabola.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="322" data-file-width="279" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_a_parabola.png/200px-Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_a_parabola.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/77/Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_a_parabola.png 1.5x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_a_parabola.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Standard forms of a parabola</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_a_hyperbola.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="244" data-file-width="321" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_a_hyperbola.png/280px-Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_a_hyperbola.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_a_hyperbola.png 1.5x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Conic_section_-_standard_forms_of_a_hyperbola.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Standard forms of a hyperbola</div></div></div>
<p>After introducing <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Cartesian coordinates</a>, the focus-directrix property can be used to produce the equations satisfied by the points of the conic section.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> By means of a change of coordinates (<a href="/wiki/Rotation_of_axes#Rotation_of_conic_sections" title="Rotation of axes">rotation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Translation_of_axes#Translation_of_conic_sections" title="Translation of axes">translation of axes</a>) these equations can be put into <i>standard forms</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> For ellipses and hyperbolas a standard form has the <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">x</span>-axis as principal axis and the origin (0,0) as center. The vertices are <span class="texhtml">(±<i>a</i>, 0)</span> and the foci <span class="texhtml">(±<i>c</i>, 0)</span>. Define <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span> by the equations <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup> = <i>a</i><sup>2</sup> − <i>b</i><sup>2</sup></span> for an ellipse and <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup> = <i>a</i><sup>2</sup> + <i>b</i><sup>2</sup></span> for a hyperbola. For a circle, <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i> = 0</span> so <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i><sup>2</sup> = <i>b</i><sup>2</sup></span>, with <i><a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a></i> <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i> = <i>a</i> = <i>b</i></span>. For the parabola, the standard form has the focus on the <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">x</span>-axis at the point <span class="texhtml">(<i>a</i>, 0)</span> and the directrix the line with equation <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i> = −<i>a</i></span>. In standard form the parabola will always pass through the origin.
</p><p>For a  <i>rectangular</i> or <i>equilateral</i> hyperbola, one whose asymptotes are perpendicular, there is an alternative standard form in which the asymptotes are the coordinate axes and the line <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i> = <i>y</i></span> is the principal axis. The foci then have coordinates <span class="texhtml">(<i>c</i>, <i>c</i>)</span> and <span class="texhtml">(−<i>c</i>, −<i>c</i>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Circle:
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}=a^{2},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<msup>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}=a^{2},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}=a^{2},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6c4fe268b7b1458ff4ed4c57d69b7e2ec2190017" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:13.468ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl></li>
<li>Ellipse:
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {x^{2}}{a^{2}}}+{\frac {y^{2}}{b^{2}}}=1,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

</mrow>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {x^{2}}{a^{2}}}+{\frac {y^{2}}{b^{2}}}=1,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\frac {x^{2}}{a^{2}}}+{\frac {y^{2}}{b^{2}}}=1,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5e04cef1c6af3e391a7fe772f38ce56bd0a71cc5" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:14.019ex; height:6.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl></li>
<li>Parabola:
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle y^{2}=4ax,{\text{ with }}a&gt;0,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>a</mi>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext> with </mtext>
</mrow>
<mi>a</mi>
<mo>&gt;</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
<mo>,</mo>

</mrow>

<li>Rectangular hyperbola:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle xy=c^{2}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>The first four of these forms are symmetric about both the <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i></span>-axis and <span class="texhtml"><i>y</i></span>-axis (for the circle, ellipse and hyperbola), or about the <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i></span>-axis only (for the parabola). The rectangular hyperbola, however, is instead symmetric about the lines <span class="texhtml"><i>y</i> = <i>x</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>y</i> = −<i>x</i></span>.
</p><p>These standard forms can be written <a href="/wiki/Parametric_equation" title="Parametric equation">parametrically</a> as,

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_Cartesian_form">General Cartesian form</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Cartesian coordinate system</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Graph_of_a_function" title="Graph of a function">graph</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_equation" title="Quadratic equation">quadratic equation</a> in two variables is always a conic section (though it may be <a href="#Degenerate_cases">degenerate</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[a]</a></sup> and all conic sections arise in this way. The most general equation is of the form<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle Ax^{2}+Bxy+Cy^{2}+Dx+Ey+F=0,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>A</mi>
<msup>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>B</mi>
<mi>x</mi>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>C</mi>
<msup>
<mi>y</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>D</mi>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>E</mi>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>F</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Ax^{2}+Bxy+Cy^{2}+Dx+Ey+F=0,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle Ax^{2}+Bxy+Cy^{2}+Dx+Ey+F=0,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/856e0676ee847892c473827a350da54170311703" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:39.393ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>with all coefficients <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">A, B, C</span> not all zero.
</p>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \left({\begin{matrix}x&amp;y&amp;1\end{matrix}}\right)\left({\begin{matrix}A&amp;B/2&amp;D/2\\B/2&amp;C&amp;E/2\\D/2&amp;E/2&amp;F\end{matrix}}\right)\left({\begin{matrix}x\\y\\1\end{matrix}}\right)=0.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \left({\begin{matrix}x&amp;y&amp;1\end{matrix}}\right)\left({\begin{matrix}A&amp;B/2&amp;D/2\\B/2&amp;C&amp;E/2\\D/2&amp;E/2&amp;F\end{matrix}}\right)\left({\begin{matrix}x\\y\\1\end{matrix}}\right)=0.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b132c62cff435a47f97683194346a1e6a01fd7c9" style="vertical-align: -4.338ex; width:44.746ex; height:9.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>This form is a specialization of the homogeneous form used in the more general setting of projective geometry (see <a href="#In_the_real_projective_plane">below</a>).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Discriminant">Discriminant</span></h4>
<p>The conic sections described by this equation can be classified in terms of the value <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle B^{2}-4AC}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>B</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>4</mn>
<mi>A</mi>
<mi>C</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle B^{2}-4AC}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="B^{2}-4AC" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/71b5fff71733e4011529beb9322373e079e4e4ca" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:10.331ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>, called the <a href="/wiki/Discriminant" title="Discriminant">discriminant</a> of the equation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>
Thus, the discriminant is <span class="texhtml">− 4Δ</span> where <span class="texhtml">Δ</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Determinant" title="Determinant">matrix determinant</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \left|{\begin{matrix}A&amp;B/2\\B/2&amp;C\end{matrix}}\right|.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow>
<mo>|</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>A</mi>
</mtd>
<mtd>
<mi>B</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mtd>
</mtr>
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>B</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mtd>
<mtd>
<mi>C</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
</mtable>
</mrow>
<mo>|</mo>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \left|{\begin{matrix}A&amp;B/2\\B/2&amp;C\end{matrix}}\right|.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \left|{\begin{matrix}A&amp;B/2\\B/2&amp;C\end{matrix}}\right|.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5c41c6bc76616f9959f7df7fb8e0604ec7d7b0d2" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:13.58ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span>
</p><p>If the conic is <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_conic" title="Degenerate conic">non-degenerate</a>, then:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Protter_1970_326_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Protter_1970_326-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>if <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i><sup>2</sup> − 4<i>AC</i> &lt; 0</span>, the equation represents an <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a>;
<ul><li>if <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i> = <i>C</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i> = 0</span>, the equation represents a <a href="/wiki/Circle" title="Circle">circle</a>, which is a special case of an ellipse;</li></ul></li>
<li>if <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i><sup>2</sup> − 4<i>AC</i> = 0</span>, the equation represents a <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a>;</li>
<li>if <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i><sup>2</sup> − 4<i>AC</i> &gt; 0</span>, the equation represents a <a href="/wiki/Hyperbola" title="Hyperbola">hyperbola</a>;
<ul><li>if <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i> + <i>C</i> = 0</span>, the equation represents a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rectangular_hyperbola" title="Rectangular hyperbola">rectangular hyperbola</a>.</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>In the notation used here, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">A</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">B</span> are polynomial coefficients, in contrast to some sources that denote the semimajor and semiminor axes as <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">A</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">B</span>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Invariants">Invariants</span></h4>
<p>The discriminant <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i><sup>2</sup> – 4<i>AC</i></span> of the conic section's quadratic equation (or equivalently the <a href="/wiki/Determinant" title="Determinant">determinant</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>AC</i> – <i>B</i><sup>2</sup>/4</span> of the 2 × 2 matrix) and the quantity <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i> + <i>C</i></span> (the <a href="/wiki/Trace_(linear_algebra)" title="Trace (linear algebra)">trace</a> of the 2 × 2 matrix) are invariant under arbitrary rotations and translations of the coordinate axes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Protter_1970_326_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Protter_1970_326-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> as is the determinant of the <a href="#Matrix_notation">3 × 3 matrix above</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spain_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spain-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: pp. 60–62">: pp. 60–62 </span></sup> The constant term <span class="texhtml"><i>F</i></span> and the sum <span class="texhtml"><i>D</i><sup>2</sup> + <i>E</i><sup>2</sup></span> are invariant under rotation only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spain_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spain-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: pp. 60–62">: pp. 60–62 </span></sup>
</p>

<p>the eccentricity can be written as a function of the coefficients of the quadratic equation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> If <span class="texhtml">4<i>AC</i> = <i>B</i><sup>2</sup></span> the conic is a parabola and its eccentricity equals 1 (provided it is non-degenerate). </p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversion_to_canonical_form">Conversion to canonical form</span></h4>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {{\tilde {x}}^{2}}{-S/(\lambda _{1}^{2}\lambda _{2})}}+{\frac {{\tilde {y}}^{2}}{-S/(\lambda _{1}\lambda _{2}^{2})}}=1,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\frac {{\tilde {x}}^{2}}{-S/(\lambda _{1}^{2}\lambda _{2})}}+{\frac {{\tilde {y}}^{2}}{-S/(\lambda _{1}\lambda _{2}^{2})}}=1,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/808b75b6f129d8e3ddb6d617e8b190b5daa1fc7f" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:31.617ex; height:6.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>

</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_coordinates">Polar coordinates</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:258px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Conics_anim.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="256" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/Conics_anim.gif" width="256"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Conics_anim.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Development of the conic section as the eccentricity <i>e</i> increases</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Polar_coordinate_system" title="Polar coordinate system">polar coordinates</a>, a conic section with one focus at the origin and, if any, the other at a negative value (for an ellipse) or a positive value (for a hyperbola) on the <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i></span>-axis, is given by the equation
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle r={\frac {l}{1+e\cos \theta }},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>r</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>l</mi>
<mrow>
<mn>1</mn>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>e</mi>
<mi>cos</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle r={\frac {l}{1+e\cos \theta }},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="r={\frac {l}{1+e\cos \theta }}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7831ca98e99a29ebffcd80f8ceb8c0ec0c3ac597" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:15.692ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i></span> is the eccentricity and <span class="texhtml"><i>l</i></span> is the semi-latus rectum.
</p><p>As above, for <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i> = 0</span>, the graph is a circle, for <span class="texhtml">0 &lt; <i>e</i> &lt; 1</span> the graph is an ellipse, for <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i> = 1</span> a parabola, and for <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i> &gt; 1</span> a hyperbola.
</p><p>The polar form of the equation of a conic is often used in <a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(mechanics)" title="Dynamics (mechanics)">dynamics</a>; for instance, determining the orbits of objects revolving about the Sun.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span></h3>
<p>Just as two (distinct) points determine a line, <a href="/wiki/Five_points_determine_a_conic" title="Five points determine a conic">five points determine a conic</a>. Formally, given any five points in the plane in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_linear_position" title="General linear position">general linear position</a>, meaning no three <a href="/wiki/Line_(geometry)" title="Line (geometry)">collinear</a>, there is a unique conic passing through them, which will be non-degenerate; this is true in both the Euclidean plane and its extension, the real projective plane. Indeed, given any five points there is a conic passing through them, but if three of the points are collinear the conic will be degenerate (reducible, because it contains a line), and may not be unique; see <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_conic#Points_to_define" title="Degenerate conic">further discussion</a>.
</p><p>Four points in the plane in general linear position determine a unique conic passing through the first three points and having the fourth point as its center. Thus knowing the center is equivalent to knowing two points on the conic for the purpose of determining the curve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WW_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-WW-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, a conic is determined by any combination of <i>k</i> points in general position that it passes through and 5 – <i>k</i> lines that are tangent to it, for 0≤<i>k</i>≤5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Any point in the plane is on either zero, one or two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tangent_line" title="Tangent line">tangent lines</a> of a conic. A point on just one tangent line is on the conic. A point on no tangent line is said to be an <i>interior point</i> (or inner point) of the conic, while a point on two tangent lines is an <i>exterior point</i> (or outer point).
</p><p>All the conic sections share a <i>reflection property</i> that can be stated as: All mirrors in the shape of a non-degenerate conic section reflect light coming from or going toward one focus toward or away from the other focus. In the case of the parabola, the second focus needs to be thought of as infinitely far away, so that the light rays going toward or coming from the second focus are parallel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_theorem" title="Pascal's theorem">Pascal's theorem</a> concerns the collinearity of three points that are constructed from a set of six points on any non-degenerate conic. The theorem also holds for degenerate conics consisting of two lines, but in that case it is known as <a href="/wiki/Pappus%27s_hexagon_theorem" title="Pappus's hexagon theorem">Pappus's theorem</a>.
</p><p>Non-degenerate conic sections are always "<a href="/wiki/Smoothness" title="Smoothness">smooth</a>". This is important for many applications, such as aerodynamics, where a smooth surface is required to ensure <a href="/wiki/Laminar_flow" title="Laminar flow">laminar flow</a> and to prevent <a href="/wiki/Turbulence" title="Turbulence">turbulence</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Menaechmus_and_early_works">Menaechmus and early works</span></h3>
<p>It is believed that the first definition of a conic section was given by <a href="/wiki/Menaechmus" title="Menaechmus">Menaechmus</a> (died 320 BC) as part of his solution of the Delian problem (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duplicating_the_cube" title="Duplicating the cube">Duplicating the cube</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> His work did not survive, not even the names he used for these curves, and is only known through secondary accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> The definition used at that time differs from the one commonly used today. Cones were constructed by rotating a right triangle about one of its legs so the hypotenuse generates the surface of the cone (such a line is called a <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/generatrix" title="wikt:generatrix">generatrix</a>). Three types of cones were determined by their vertex angles (measured by twice the angle formed by the hypotenuse and the leg being rotated about in the right triangle). The conic section was then determined by intersecting one of these cones with a plane drawn perpendicular to a generatrix. The type of the conic is determined by the type of cone, that is, by the angle formed at the vertex of the cone: If the angle is acute then the conic is an ellipse; if the angle is right then the conic is a parabola; and if the angle is obtuse then the conic is a hyperbola (but only one branch of the curve).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> (fl. 300 BC) is said to have written four books on conics but these were lost as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> (died <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 212</span> BC) is known to have studied conics, having determined the area bounded by a parabola and a chord in <i>Quadrature of the Parabola</i>. His main interest was in terms of measuring areas and volumes of figures related to the conics and part of this work survives in his book on the solids of revolution of conics, <i>On Conoids and Spheroids</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apollonius_of_Perga">Apollonius of Perga</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Conica_of_Apollonius_of_Perga_fol._6b-7a_DETAIL.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="241" data-file-width="388" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Conica_of_Apollonius_of_Perga_fol._6b-7a_DETAIL.jpg/280px-Conica_of_Apollonius_of_Perga_fol._6b-7a_DETAIL.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/40/Conica_of_Apollonius_of_Perga_fol._6b-7a_DETAIL.jpg 1.5x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Conica_of_Apollonius_of_Perga_fol._6b-7a_DETAIL.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram from Apollonius' <i>Conics</i>, in a 9th-century Arabic translation</div></div></div>
<p>The greatest progress in the study of conics by the ancient Greeks is due to <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Perga" title="Apollonius of Perga">Apollonius of Perga</a> (died <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 190</span> BC), whose eight-volume <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Perga#Conics" title="Apollonius of Perga"><i>Conic Sections</i> or <i>Conics</i></a> summarized and greatly extended existing knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> Apollonius's study of the properties of these curves made it possible to show that any plane cutting a fixed double cone (two napped), regardless of its angle, will produce a conic according to the earlier definition, leading to the definition commonly used today. Circles, not constructible by the earlier method, are also obtainable in this way. This may account for why Apollonius considered circles a fourth type of conic section, a distinction that is no longer made. Apollonius used the names 'ellipse', 'parabola' and 'hyperbola' for these curves, borrowing the terminology from earlier Pythagorean work on areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pappus_of_Alexandria" title="Pappus of Alexandria">Pappus of Alexandria</a> (died <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 350</span> AD) is credited with expounding on the importance of the concept of a conic's focus, and detailing the related concept of a <a href="#Eccentricity,_focus_and_directrix">directrix</a>, including the case of the parabola (which is lacking in Apollonius's known works).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Al-Kuhi">Al-Kuhi</span></h3>
<p>An instrument for drawing conic sections was first described in 1000 AD by the Islamic mathematician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Sahl_al-Q%C5%ABh%C4%AB" title="Abū Sahl al-Qūhī">Al-Kuhi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Omar_Khayy.C3.A1m"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Omar_Khayyám">Omar Khayyám</span></h3>
<p>Apollonius's work was translated into Arabic, and much of his work only survives through the Arabic version. Persians found applications of the theory, most notably the Persian mathematician and poet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omar_Khayy%C3%A1m" title="Omar Khayyám">Omar Khayyám</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> who found a geometrical method of solving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cubic_equations" title="Cubic equations">cubic equations</a> using conic sections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> extended the theory of conics through the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smooth_function#Geometric_continuity" title="Smooth function">principle of continuity</a>", a precursor to the concept of limits. Kepler first used the term 'foci' in 1604.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Girard_Desargues" title="Girard Desargues">Girard Desargues</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Blaise Pascal</a> developed a theory of conics using an early form of <a href="/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a> and this helped to provide impetus for the study of this new field. In particular, Pascal discovered a theorem known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexagrammum_mysticum" title="Hexagrammum mysticum">hexagrammum mysticum</a> from which many other properties of conics can be deduced.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pierre_Fermat" title="Pierre Fermat">Pierre Fermat</a> both applied their newly discovered <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">analytic geometry</a> to the study of conics. This had the effect of reducing the geometrical problems of conics to problems in algebra. However, it was <a href="/wiki/John_Wallis" title="John Wallis">John Wallis</a> in his 1655 treatise <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Tractatus de sectionibus conicis</i></span> who first defined the conic sections as instances of equations of second degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> Written earlier, but published later, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jan_de_Witt" title="Jan de Witt">Jan de Witt</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Elementa Curvarum Linearum</i></span> starts with Kepler's <a href="/wiki/Kinematics" title="Kinematics">kinematic</a> construction of the conics and then develops the algebraic equations. This work, which uses Fermat's methodology and Descartes' notation has been described as the first textbook on the subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BoyerDeWitt_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-BoyerDeWitt-42">[40]</a></sup> De Witt invented the term 'directrix'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BoyerDeWitt_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-BoyerDeWitt-42">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For specific applications of each type of conic section, see <a href="/wiki/Circle" title="Circle">Circle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">Ellipse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">Parabola</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hyperbola" title="Hyperbola">Hyperbola</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archeocyathids.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Archeocyathids.JPG/220px-Archeocyathids.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Archeocyathids.JPG/330px-Archeocyathids.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Archeocyathids.JPG/440px-Archeocyathids.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archeocyathids.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Paraboloid" title="Paraboloid">paraboloid</a> shape of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archeocyathid" title="Archeocyathid">Archeocyathids</a> produces conic sections on rock faces</div></div></div>
<p>Conic sections are important in <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a> of two massive objects that interact according to <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">Newton's law of universal gravitation</a> are conic sections if their common <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a> is considered to be at rest. If they are bound together, they will both trace out ellipses; if they are moving apart, they will both follow parabolas or hyperbolas. See <a href="/wiki/Two-body_problem" title="Two-body problem">two-body problem</a>.
</p><p>The reflective properties of the conic sections are used in the design of searchlights, radio-telescopes and some optical telescopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> A searchlight uses a parabolic mirror as the reflector, with a bulb at the focus; and a similar construction is used for a <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_microphone" title="Parabolic microphone">parabolic microphone</a>. The 4.2 meter <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel_Telescope" title="William Herschel Telescope">Herschel optical telescope</a> on La Palma, in the Canary islands, uses a primary parabolic mirror to reflect light towards a secondary hyperbolic mirror, which reflects it again to a focus behind the first mirror.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_real_projective_plane">In the real projective plane</span></h2>
<p>The conic sections have some very similar properties in the Euclidean plane and the reasons for this become clearer when the conics are viewed from the perspective of a larger geometry. The Euclidean plane may be embedded in the <a href="/wiki/Real_projective_plane" title="Real projective plane">real projective plane</a> and the conics may be considered as objects in this projective geometry. One way to do this is to introduce <a href="/wiki/Homogeneous_coordinates" title="Homogeneous coordinates">homogeneous coordinates</a> and define a conic to be the set of points whose coordinates satisfy an irreducible quadratic equation in three variables (or equivalently, the zeros of an irreducible <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_form" title="Quadratic form">quadratic form</a>). More technically, the set of points that are zeros of a quadratic form (in any number of variables) is called a <a href="/wiki/Quadric" title="Quadric">quadric</a>, and the irreducible quadrics in a two dimensional projective space (that is, having three variables) are traditionally called conics.
</p><p>The Euclidean plane <span class="texhtml"><i>R</i><sup>2</sup></span> is embedded in the real projective plane by adjoining a <a href="/wiki/Line_at_infinity" title="Line at infinity">line at infinity</a> (and its corresponding <a href="/wiki/Point_at_infinity" title="Point at infinity">points at infinity</a>) so that all the lines of a parallel class meet on this line. On the other hand, starting with the real projective plane, a Euclidean plane is obtained by distinguishing some line as the line at infinity and removing it and all its points.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intersection_at_infinity">Intersection at infinity</span></h3>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Projective_space" title="Projective space">projective space</a> over any division ring, but in particular over either the real or complex numbers, all non-degenerate conics are equivalent, and thus in projective geometry one simply speaks of "a conic" without specifying a type. That is, there is a projective transformation that will map any non-degenerate conic to any other non-degenerate conic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The three types of conic sections will reappear in the affine plane obtained by choosing a line of the projective space to be the line at infinity. The three types are then determined by how this line at infinity intersects the conic in the projective space. In the corresponding affine space, one obtains an ellipse if the conic does not intersect the line at infinity, a parabola if the conic intersects the line at infinity in one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_point" title="Double point">double point</a> corresponding to the axis, and a hyperbola if the conic intersects the line at infinity in two points corresponding to the asymptotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p>

</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Projective_definition_of_a_circle">Projective definition of a circle</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Metric_space" title="Metric space">Metrical</a> concepts of Euclidean geometry (concepts concerned with measuring lengths and angles) can not be immediately extended to the real projective plane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[d]</a></sup> They must be redefined (and generalized) in this new geometry. This can be done for arbitrary <a href="/wiki/Projective_plane" title="Projective plane">projective planes</a>, but to obtain the real projective plane as the extended Euclidean plane, some specific choices have to be made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fix an arbitrary line in a projective plane that shall be referred to as the <i>absolute line</i>. Select two distinct points on the absolute line and refer to them as <i>absolute points</i>. Several metrical concepts can be defined with reference to these choices. For instance, given a line containing the points <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">A</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">B</span>, the <i>midpoint</i> of line segment <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">AB</span> is defined as the point <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">C</span> which is the <a href="/wiki/Projective_harmonic_conjugate" title="Projective harmonic conjugate">projective harmonic conjugate</a> of the point of intersection of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">AB</span> and the absolute line, with respect to <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">A</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">B</span>.
</p><p>A conic in a projective plane that contains the two absolute points is called a <i>circle</i>. Since five points determine a conic, a circle (which may be degenerate) is determined by three points. To obtain the extended Euclidean plane, the absolute line is chosen to be the line at infinity of the Euclidean plane and the absolute points are two special points on that line called the <a href="/wiki/Circular_points_at_infinity" title="Circular points at infinity">circular points at infinity</a>. Lines containing two points with real coordinates do not pass through the circular points at infinity, so in the Euclidean plane a circle, under this definition, is determined by three points that are not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collinear" title="Collinear">collinear</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been mentioned that circles in the Euclidean plane can not be defined by the focus-directrix property. However, if one were to consider the line at infinity as the directrix, then by taking the eccentricity to be <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i> = 0</span> a circle will have the focus-directrix property, but it is still not defined by that property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[46]</a></sup> One must be careful in this situation to correctly use the definition of eccentricity as the ratio of the distance of a point on the circle to the focus (length of a radius) to the distance of that point to the directrix (this distance is infinite) which gives the limiting value of zero.
</p>
<h3><span id="Steiner.27s_projective_conic_definition"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Steiner's_projective_conic_definition">Steiner's projective conic definition</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Steiner_conic" title="Steiner conic">Steiner conic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Steiner-erz-def-s.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="271" data-file-width="224" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Steiner-erz-def-s.svg/220px-Steiner-erz-def-s.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Steiner-erz-def-s.svg/330px-Steiner-erz-def-s.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Steiner-erz-def-s.svg/440px-Steiner-erz-def-s.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Steiner-erz-def-s.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Definition of the Steiner generation of a conic section</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_geometry" title="Synthetic geometry">synthetic</a> (coordinate-free) approach to defining the conic sections in a projective plane was given by <a href="/wiki/Jakob_Steiner" title="Jakob Steiner">Jakob Steiner</a> in 1867.
</p>
