</p><p>A <i>projective</i> mapping is a finite sequence of perspective mappings.
</p><p>As a projective mapping in a projective plane over a field (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pappian_plane" title="Pappian plane">pappian plane</a>) is uniquely determined by prescribing the images of three lines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup> for the Steiner generation of a conic section, besides two points <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle U,V}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>U</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>V</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle U,V}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="U,V" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7681409ec5fffdb272f536757c1211fe0151a9b2" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:4.604ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> only the images of 3 lines have to be given. These 5 items (2 points, 3 lines) uniquely determine the conic section.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Line_conics">Line conics</span></h3>
<p>By the <a href="/wiki/Duality_(projective_geometry)" title="Duality (projective geometry)">Principle of Duality</a> in a projective plane, the dual of each point is a line, and the dual of a locus of points (a set of points satisfying some condition) is called an <i>envelope</i> of lines. Using Steiner's definition of a conic (this locus of points will now be referred to as a <i>point conic</i>) as the meet of corresponding rays of two related pencils, it is easy to dualize and obtain the corresponding envelope consisting of the joins of corresponding points of two related ranges (points on a line) on different bases (the lines the points are on). Such an envelope is called a <i>line conic</i> (or dual conic).
</p><p>In the real projective plane, a point conic has the property that every line meets it in two points (which may coincide, or may be complex) and any set of points with this property is a point conic. It follows dually that a line conic has two of its lines through every point and any envelope of lines with this property is a line conic. At every point of a point conic there is a unique tangent line, and dually, on every line of a line conic there is a unique point called a <i>point of contact</i>. An important theorem states that the tangent lines of a point conic form a line conic, and dually, the points of contact of a line conic form a point conic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Von_Staudt.27s_definition"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Von_Staudt's_definition">Von Staudt's definition</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Von_Staudt_conic" title="Von Staudt conic">Von Staudt conic</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Karl_Georg_Christian_von_Staudt" title="Karl Georg Christian von Staudt">Karl Georg Christian von Staudt</a> defined a conic as the point set given by all the absolute points of a <i>polarity</i> that has absolute points. Von Staudt introduced this definition in <i>Geometrie der Lage</i> (1847) as part of his attempt to remove all metrical concepts from projective geometry.
</p><p>A <i>polarity</i>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, of a projective plane <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><i>P</i></span> is an <a href="/wiki/Involution_(mathematics)" title="Involution (mathematics)">involutory</a> <a href="/wiki/Bijection" title="Bijection">bijection</a> between the points and the lines of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><i>P</i></span> that preserves the <a href="/wiki/Incidence_(geometry)" title="Incidence (geometry)">incidence relation</a>. Thus, a polarity associates a point <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><i>Q</i></span> with a line <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><i>q</i></span> by <span class="texhtml"><i>π</i>(<i>Q</i>) = <i>q</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>π</i>(<i>q</i>) = <i>Q</i></span>. Following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gergonne" title="Gergonne">Gergonne</a>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><i>q</i></span> is called the <a href="/wiki/Pole_and_polar" title="Pole and polar"><i>polar</i></a> of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><i>Q</i></span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><i>Q</i></span> the <a href="/wiki/Pole_and_polar" title="Pole and polar"><i>pole</i></a> of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><i>q</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup> An <i>absolute point</i> (or <i>line</i>) of a polarity is one which is incident with its polar (pole).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[e]</a></sup>
</p><p>A von Staudt conic in the real projective plane is equivalent to a <a href="/wiki/Steiner_conic" title="Steiner conic">Steiner conic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Constructions">Constructions</span></h3>
<p>No continuous arc of a conic can be constructed with straightedge and compass. However, there are several straightedge-and-compass constructions for any number of individual points on an arc.
</p><p>One of them is based on the converse of Pascal's theorem, namely, <i>if the points of intersection of opposite sides of a hexagon are collinear, then the six vertices lie on a conic.</i> Specifically, given five points, <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i>, <i>B</i>, <i>C</i>, <i>D</i>, <i>E</i></span> and a line passing through <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">E</span>, say <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>EG</i></span></span>, a point <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">F</span> that lies on this line and is on the conic determined by the five points can be constructed. Let <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>AB</i></span></span> meet <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>DE</i></span></span> in <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">L</span>, <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>BC</i></span></span> meet <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>EG</i></span></span> in <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">M</span> and let <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>CD</i></span></span> meet <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>LM</i></span></span> at <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">N</span>. Then <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>AN</i></span></span> meets <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>EG</i></span></span> at the required point <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">F</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup> By varying the line through <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">E</span>,as many additional points on the conic as desired can be constructed.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ellipse_construction_-_parallelogram_method.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Ellipse_construction_-_parallelogram_method.gif/220px-Ellipse_construction_-_parallelogram_method.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Ellipse_construction_-_parallelogram_method.gif/330px-Ellipse_construction_-_parallelogram_method.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/Ellipse_construction_-_parallelogram_method.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ellipse_construction_-_parallelogram_method.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Parallelogram method for constructing an ellipse</div></div></div>
<p>Another method, based on Steiner's construction and which is useful in engineering applications, is the parallelogram method, where a conic is constructed point by point by means of connecting certain equally spaced points on a horizontal line and a vertical line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[56]</a></sup> Specifically, to construct the ellipse with equation <span class="texhtml"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1050945101">.mw-parser-output .sfrac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .sfrac.tion,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .tion{display:inline-block;vertical-align:-0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .num,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{display:block;line-height:1em;margin:0 0.1em}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{border-top:1px solid}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>x</i><sup>2</sup></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><i>a</i><sup>2</sup></span></span> + <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>y</i><sup>2</sup></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><i>b</i><sup>2</sup></span></span> = 1</span>, first construct the rectangle <span class="texhtml"><i>ABCD</i></span> with vertices <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i>(<i>a</i>, 0), <i>B</i>(<i>a</i>, 2<i>b</i>), <i>C</i>(−<i>a</i>, 2<i>b</i>)</span> and  <span class="texhtml"><i>D</i>(−<i>a</i>, 0)</span>. Divide the side <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>BC</i></span></span> into <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">n</span> equal segments and use parallel projection, with respect to the diagonal <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>AC</i></span></span>, to form equal segments on side <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>AB</i></span></span> (the lengths of these segments will be <span class="texhtml"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>b</i></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><i>a</i></span></span></span> times the length of the segments on <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>BC</i></span></span>). On the side <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>BC</i></span></span> label the left-hand endpoints of the segments with <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i><sub>1</sub></span> to <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i><sub>n</sub></span> starting at <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">B</span> and going towards <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">C</span>. On the side <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>AB</i></span></span> label the upper endpoints <span class="texhtml"><i>D</i><sub>1</sub></span> to <span class="texhtml"><i>D</i><sub>n</sub></span> starting at <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">A</span> and going towards <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">B</span>. The points of intersection, <span class="texhtml"><span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>AA</i></span><sub>i</sub> ∩ <span style="text-decoration:overline;"><i>DD</i></span><sub>i</sub></span> for <span class="texhtml">1 ≤ <i>i</i> ≤ <i>n</i></span> will be points of the ellipse between <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">A</span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>P</i>(0, <i>b</i>)</span>. The labeling associates the lines of the pencil through <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">A</span> with the lines of the pencil through <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">D</span> projectively but not perspectively. The sought for conic is obtained by this construction since three points <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i>, <i>D</i></span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">P</span> and two tangents (the vertical lines at <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">A</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">D</span>) uniquely determine the conic. If another diameter (and its conjugate diameter) are used instead of the major and minor axes of the ellipse, a parallelogram that is not a rectangle is used in the construction, giving the name of the method. The association of lines of the pencils can be extended to obtain other points on the ellipse. The constructions for hyperbolas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[57]</a></sup> and parabolas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[58]</a></sup> are similar.
</p><p>Yet another general method uses the polarity property to construct the tangent envelope of a conic (a line conic).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_complex_projective_plane">In the complex projective plane</span></h2>
<p>In the complex plane <span class="texhtml"><b>C</b><sup>2</sup></span>, ellipses and hyperbolas are not distinct: one may consider a hyperbola as an ellipse with an imaginary axis length. For example, the ellipse <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}=1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<msup>
<mi>y</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}=1}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x^{2}+y^{2}=1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ec84b90236512e8d27ff1a8f7707b60b63327de1" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:11.7ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> becomes a hyperbola under the substitution <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle y=iw,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>y</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>w</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle y=iw,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="y=iw," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/499e2908993a670dcd82beca4e4c7f69e480f0f0" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:7.367ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> geometrically a complex rotation, yielding <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x^{2}-w^{2}=1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<msup>
<mi>w</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x^{2}-w^{2}=1}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x^{2}-w^{2}=1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2a89556245fc3745166df28059b531aecc22fd81" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:12.204ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>. Thus there is a 2-way classification: ellipse/hyperbola and parabola. Extending the curves to the complex projective plane, this corresponds to intersecting the <a href="/wiki/Line_at_infinity" title="Line at infinity">line at infinity</a> in either 2 distinct points (corresponding to two asymptotes) or in 1 double point (corresponding to the axis of a parabola); thus the real hyperbola is a more suggestive real image for the complex ellipse/hyperbola, as it also has 2 (real) intersections with the line at infinity.
</p><p>Further unification occurs in the <a href="/wiki/Complex_projective_plane" title="Complex projective plane">complex projective plane</a> <span class="texhtml"><b>CP</b><sup>2</sup></span>: the non-degenerate conics cannot be distinguished from one another, since any can be taken to any other by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Projective_linear_transformation" title="Projective linear transformation">projective linear transformation</a>.
</p><p>It can be proven that in <span class="texhtml"><b>CP</b><sup>2</sup></span>, two conic sections have four points in common (if one accounts for <a href="/wiki/Multiplicity_(mathematics)" title="Multiplicity (mathematics)">multiplicity</a>), so there are between 1 and 4 <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9zout%27s_theorem" title="Bézout's theorem">intersection</a> points. The intersection possibilities are: four distinct points, two singular points and one double point, two double points, one singular point and one with multiplicity 3, one point with multiplicity 4. If any intersection point has multiplicity &gt; 1, the two curves are said to be <a href="/wiki/Tangent" title="Tangent">tangent</a>. If there is an intersection point of multiplicity at least 3, the two curves are said to be <a href="/wiki/Osculating_curve" title="Osculating curve">osculating</a>. If there is only one intersection point, which has multiplicity 4, the two curves are said to be <i>superosculating</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, each <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Straight_line" title="Straight line">straight line</a> intersects each conic section twice. If the intersection point is double, the line is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tangent_line" title="Tangent line">tangent line</a>.
Intersecting with the line at infinity, each conic section has two points at infinity. If these points are real, the curve is a <a href="/wiki/Hyperbola" title="Hyperbola">hyperbola</a>; if they are imaginary conjugates, it is an <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a>; if there is only one double point, it is a <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a>. If the points at infinity are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclic_points" title="Cyclic points">cyclic points</a> <span class="texhtml">(1, <i>i</i>, 0)</span> and <span class="texhtml">(1, –<i>i</i>, 0)</span>, the conic section is a <a href="/wiki/Circle" title="Circle">circle</a>. If the coefficients of a conic section are real, the points at infinity are either real or <a href="/wiki/Complex_conjugate" title="Complex conjugate">complex conjugate</a>.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Degenerate_conics"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Degenerate_cases">Degenerate cases</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_conic" title="Degenerate conic">Degenerate conic</a></div>
<p>What should be considered as a <i>degenerate</i> case of a conic depends on the definition being used and the geometric setting for the conic section. There are some authors who define a conic as a two-dimensional nondegenerate quadric. With this terminology there are no degenerate conics (only degenerate quadrics), but we shall use the more traditional terminology and avoid that definition.
</p><p>In the Euclidean plane, using the geometric definition, a degenerate case arises when the cutting plane passes through the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/apex" title="wikt:apex">apex</a> of the cone.
The degenerate conic is either: a <a href="/wiki/Point_(geometry)" title="Point (geometry)">point</a>, when the plane intersects the cone only at the apex; a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Straight_line" title="Straight line">straight line</a>, when the plane is tangent to the cone (it contains exactly one generator of the cone); or a pair of intersecting lines (two generators of the cone).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[61]</a></sup> These correspond respectively to the limiting forms of an ellipse, parabola, and a hyperbola.
</p><p>If a conic in the Euclidean plane is being defined by the zeros of a quadratic equation (that is, as a quadric), then the degenerate conics are: the <a href="/wiki/Empty_set" title="Empty set">empty set</a>, a point, or a pair of lines which may be parallel, intersect at a point, or coincide. The empty set case may correspond either to a pair of <a href="/wiki/Complex_conjugate" title="Complex conjugate">complex conjugate</a> parallel lines such as with the equation <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x^{2}+1=0,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x^{2}+1=0,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle x^{2}+1=0,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6eefe27838566005bb9c66404d2d74ae699f9b72" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:11.295ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> or to an <i>imaginary ellipse</i>, such as with the equation <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}+1=0.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<msup>
<mi>y</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0.</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}+1=0.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}+1=0.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7f34ebb67cb66b8c4cafbec751f48200daf0927c" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:16.35ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> An imaginary ellipse does not satisfy the general definition of a <a href="/wiki/Degeneracy_(mathematics)" title="Degeneracy (mathematics)">degeneracy</a>, and is thus not normally considered as degenerated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[62]</a></sup> The two lines case occurs when the quadratic expression factors into two linear factors, the zeros of each giving a line. In the case that the factors are the same, the corresponding lines coincide and we refer to the line as
a <i>double</i> line (a line with <a href="/wiki/Multiplicity_(mathematics)" title="Multiplicity (mathematics)">multiplicity</a> 2) and this is the previous case of a tangent cutting plane.
</p><p>In the real projective plane, since parallel lines meet at a point on the line at infinity, the parallel line case of the Euclidean plane can be viewed as intersecting lines. However, as the point of intersection is the apex of the cone, the cone itself degenerates to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cylinder_(geometry)" title="Cylinder (geometry)">cylinder</a>, i.e. with the apex at infinity. Other sections in this case are called <i>cylindric sections</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[63]</a></sup> The non-degenerate cylindrical sections are ellipses (or circles).
</p><p>When viewed from the perspective of the complex projective plane, the degenerate cases of a real quadric (i.e., the quadratic equation has real coefficients) can all be considered as a pair of lines, possibly coinciding. The empty set may be the line at infinity considered as a double line, a (real) point is the intersection of two <a href="/wiki/Complex_conjugate_line" title="Complex conjugate line">complex conjugate lines</a> and the other cases as previously mentioned.
</p><p>To distinguish the degenerate cases from the non-degenerate cases (including the empty set with the latter) using matrix notation, let <span class="texhtml"><i>β</i></span> be the determinant of the 3 × 3 matrix of the conic section—that is, <span class="texhtml"><i>β </i> = (<i>AC</i> − <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>B</i><sup>2</sup></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">4</span></span>)<i>F</i> + <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>BED</i> − <i>CD</i><sup>2</sup> − <i>AE</i><sup>2</sup></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">4</span></span></span>; and let <span class="texhtml"><i>α</i> = <i>B</i><sup>2</sup> − 4<i>AC</i></span> be the discriminant. Then the conic section is non-degenerate if and only if <span class="texhtml"><i>β</i> ≠ 0</span>.  If <span class="texhtml"><i>β </i>= 0</span> we have a point when <span class="texhtml"><i>α</i> &lt; 0</span>, two parallel lines (possibly coinciding) when <span class="texhtml"><i>α</i> = 0</span>, or two intersecting lines when <span class="texhtml"><i>α</i> &gt; 0</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pencil_of_conics">Pencil of conics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pencil_(mathematics)#Pencil_of_conics" title="Pencil (mathematics)">Pencil (mathematics) § Pencil of conics</a></div>
<p>A (non-degenerate) conic is completely determined by <a href="/wiki/Five_points_determine_a_conic" title="Five points determine a conic">five points</a> in general position (no three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collinear" title="Collinear">collinear</a>) in a plane and the system of conics which pass through a fixed set of four points (again in a plane and no three collinear) is called a <i>pencil of conics</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup> The four common points are called the <i>base points</i> of the pencil. Through any point other than a base point, there passes a single conic of the pencil. This concept generalizes a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pencil_(mathematics)#Pencil_of_circles" title="Pencil (mathematics)">pencil of circles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Intersecting_two_conics">Intersecting two conics</span></h2>
<p>The solutions to a system of two second degree equations in two variables may be viewed as the coordinates of the points of intersection of two generic conic sections.
In particular two conics may possess none, two or four possibly coincident intersection points.
An efficient method of locating these solutions exploits the homogeneous <a href="/wiki/Matrix_representation_of_conic_sections" title="Matrix representation of conic sections">matrix representation of conic sections</a>, i.e. a 3 × 3 <a href="/wiki/Symmetric_matrix" title="Symmetric matrix">symmetric matrix</a> which depends on six parameters.
</p><p>The procedure to locate the intersection points follows these steps, where the conics are represented by matrices:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[67]</a></sup>
</p>

</math></span><img alt="\lambda " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b43d0ea3c9c025af1be9128e62a18fa74bedda2a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.355ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.402ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. These turn out to be the solutions of a third degree equation.</li>
<li>given the degenerate conic <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C_{0}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="C_{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/23da38e31194b9ae0524ec18c8489693f3be5389" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.716ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, identify the two, possibly coincident, lines constituting it.</li>
<li>intersect each identified line with either one of the two original conics; this step can be done efficiently using the dual conic representation of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<li>the points of intersection will represent the solutions to the initial equation system.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Generalizations">Generalizations</span></h2>
<p>Conics may be defined over other fields (that is, in other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pappian_plane" title="Pappian plane">pappian geometries</a>).  However, some care must be used when the field has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Characteristic_(field)" title="Characteristic (field)">characteristic</a> 2, as some formulas can not be used. For example, the matrix representations used <a href="#General_Cartesian_form">above</a> require division by 2.
</p><p>A generalization of a non-degenerate conic in a projective plane is an <a href="/wiki/Oval_(projective_plane)" title="Oval (projective plane)">oval</a>. An oval is a point set that has the following properties, which are held by conics: 1) any line intersects an oval in none, one or two points, 2) at any point of the oval there exists a unique tangent line.
</p><p>Generalizing the focus properties of conics to the case where there are more than two foci produces sets called <a href="/wiki/Generalized_conic" title="Generalized conic">generalized conics</a>.
</p><p>The intersection of an <a href="/wiki/Cone#Elliptic_cone" title="Cone">elliptic cone</a> with a sphere is a <a href="/wiki/Spherical_conic" title="Spherical conic">spherical conic</a>, which shares many properties with planar conics.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_other_areas_of_mathematics">In other areas of mathematics</span></h2>
<p>The classification into elliptic, parabolic, and hyperbolic is pervasive in mathematics, and often divides a field into sharply distinct subfields. The classification mostly arises due to the presence of a quadratic form (in two variables this corresponds to the associated <a href="/wiki/Discriminant" title="Discriminant">discriminant</a>), but can also correspond to eccentricity.
</p><p>Quadratic form classifications:
</p>
<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Quadratic_form" title="Quadratic form">Quadratic forms</a></dt>
<dd>Quadratic forms over the reals are classified by <a href="/wiki/Sylvester%27s_law_of_inertia" title="Sylvester's law of inertia">Sylvester's law of inertia</a>, namely by their positive index, zero index, and negative index: a quadratic form in <i>n</i> variables can be converted to a <a href="/wiki/Diagonal_form" title="Diagonal form">diagonal form</a>, as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x_{1}^{2}+x_{2}^{2}+\cdots +x_{k}^{2}-x_{k+1}^{2}-\cdots -x_{k+\ell }^{2},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<dd>In two variables quadratic forms are classified by discriminant, analogously to conics, but in higher dimensions the more useful classification is as <i>definite,</i> (all positive or all negative), <i>degenerate,</i> (some zeros), or <i>indefinite</i> (mix of positive and negative but no zeros). This classification underlies many that follow.</dd>
<dt>Curvature</dt>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_curvature" title="Gaussian curvature">Gaussian curvature</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Surface_(mathematics)" title="Surface (mathematics)">surface</a> describes the infinitesimal geometry, and may at each point be either positive – <a href="/wiki/Elliptic_geometry" title="Elliptic geometry">elliptic geometry</a>, zero – <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a> (flat, parabola), or negative – <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_geometry" title="Hyperbolic geometry">hyperbolic geometry</a>; infinitesimally, to second order the surface looks like the graph of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<msup>
<mi>y</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x^{2}+y^{2}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6914c006c1f216efb3c4fc5ac7b358c51da514be" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.086ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

Indeed, by the <a href="/wiki/Uniformization_theorem" title="Uniformization theorem">uniformization theorem</a> every surface can be taken to be globally (at every point) positively curved, flat, or negatively curved. In higher dimensions the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_curvature_tensor" title="Riemann curvature tensor">Riemann curvature tensor</a> is a more complicated object, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manifolds_with_constant_sectional_curvature" title="Manifolds with constant sectional curvature">manifolds with constant sectional curvature</a> are interesting objects of study, and have strikingly different properties, as discussed at <a href="/wiki/Sectional_curvature" title="Sectional curvature">sectional curvature</a>.</dd>
<dt>Second order PDEs</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Partial_differential_equation" title="Partial differential equation">Partial differential equations</a> (PDEs) of <a href="/wiki/Partial_differential_equation#Linear_equations_of_second_order" title="Partial differential equation">second order</a> are classified at each point as elliptic, parabolic, or hyperbolic, accordingly as their second order terms correspond to an elliptic, parabolic, or hyperbolic quadratic form. The behavior and theory of these different types of PDEs are strikingly different – representative examples is that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poisson_equation" title="Poisson equation">Poisson equation</a> is elliptic, the <a href="/wiki/Heat_equation" title="Heat equation">heat equation</a> is parabolic, and the <a href="/wiki/Wave_equation" title="Wave equation">wave equation</a> is hyperbolic.</dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(mathematics)#Analogous_classifications" title="Eccentricity (mathematics)">Eccentricity classifications</a> include:
</p>
<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6bius_transformation" title="Möbius transformation">Möbius transformations</a></dt>

<dt><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Variance-to-mean_ratio" title="Variance-to-mean ratio">Variance-to-mean ratio</a></dt>
<dd>The variance-to-mean ratio classifies several important families of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discrete_probability_distribution" title="Discrete probability distribution">discrete probability distributions</a>: the constant distribution as circular (eccentricity 0), <a href="/wiki/Binomial_distribution" title="Binomial distribution">binomial distributions</a> as elliptical, <a href="/wiki/Poisson_distribution" title="Poisson distribution">Poisson distributions</a> as parabolic, and <a href="/wiki/Negative_binomial_distribution" title="Negative binomial distribution">negative binomial distributions</a> as hyperbolic. This is elaborated at <a href="/wiki/Cumulant#Cumulants_of_some_discrete_probability_distributions" title="Cumulant">cumulants of some discrete probability distributions</a>.</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/Conic_section_interactive_visualisation.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Conic_section_interactive_visualisation.svg/220px-Conic_section_interactive_visualisation.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Conic_section_interactive_visualisation.svg/330px-Conic_section_interactive_visualisation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Conic_section_interactive_visualisation.svg/440px-Conic_section_interactive_visualisation.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Conic_section_interactive_visualisation.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In <a href="/wiki/Special:FilePath/Conic_section_interactive_visualisation.svg" title="Special:FilePath/Conic section interactive visualisation.svg">this interactive SVG</a>, move left and right over the SVG image to rotate the double cone</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The empty set is included as a degenerate conic, since it may arise as a solution of this equation.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, this solution was rejected by Plato on the grounds that it could not be achieved using only straightedge and compass, however this interpretation of Plutarch's statement has come under criticism. <a href="#CITEREFBoyer2004">Boyer 2004</a>, p.14, footnote 14.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This form of the equation does not generalize to fields of characteristic two.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Consider finding the midpoint of a line segment with one endpoint on the line at infinity.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Coxeter and several other authors use the term 'self-conjugate' instead of 'absolute'.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
